  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

        state: 'apple_oauth_state_949f0976-fee9-415d-931a-04b72b8560c8'
      });

<span id="freeTextContainer9109208464061203041">The most comprehensive English-language collection of work ever by "the greatest poet of the twentieth century - in any language" - Gabriel García Márquez<br/><br/>"In his work a continent awakens to consciousness." So wrote the Swedish Academy in awarding the Nobel Prize to Pablo Neruda, the author of more than thirty-five books of poetry and one of Latin America's most revered wri</span>
  <span id="freeText9109208464061203041" style="display:none">The most comprehensive English-language collection of work ever by "the greatest poet of the twentieth century - in any language" - Gabriel García Márquez<br /><br />"In his work a continent awakens to consciousness." So wrote the Swedish Academy in awarding the Nobel Prize to Pablo Neruda, the author of more than thirty-five books of poetry and one of Latin America's most revered writers, lionized during his lifetime as "the people's poet." This selection of Neruda's poetry, the most comprehensive single volume available in English, presents nearly six hundred poems. Scores of them are in new and sometimes multiple translations, and many accompanied by the Spanish original. In his introduction, Ilan Stavans situates Neruda in his native milieu as well as in a contemporary English-language one, and a group of new translations by leading poets testifies to Neruda's enduring, vibrant legacy among English-speaking writers and readers today.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9109208464061203041" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

  <span id="freeText14886837209749464484" style="display:none">Pending a review<br /><br /><b>Ute Lemper</b><br /><br />One of my favourite singers has just released an album of songs she created from 12 of Pablo Neruda poems. It's called "Forever":<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.utelemper.com/neruda/">http://www.utelemper.com/neruda/</a><br /><br /><br /><br /><b>The Flight from Weimar to Chile<br />[Ute Lemper, Live at the Concert Hall, QPAC, Brisbane, Friday, September 13, 2013]</b><br /><br />His legacy is <br />An ocean of<br />Probabilities,<br />Made likely<br />By the flow<br />Of verse<br />From its source,<br />His mind,<br />To a remote<br />Destination<br />Across the world,<br />Us, the audience<br />He had in mind,<br />Focussed and<br />Inexorable.<br /><br />You, Ute, <br />Hovered<br />Bird-like in <br />Some crazy, <br />Jazz Birdland, <br />Scatting, <br />Above the water, <br />Swooping <br />And squawking<br />And growling <br />And soaring<br />Like a flight<br />Of sea-birds<br />Over timeless<br />Slow moving<br />Estuaries.<br /><br />One by one,<br />You singled out <br />The crafted<br />Sensations<br />Of his rhyme, <br />Like gulls plunging on<br />Chips left behind<br />In the beach sand.<br />You mimicked <br />Miles’ trumpet<br />With your voice,<br />Deftly painting <br />Sketches of Spain<br />And Chile<br />In Spanish, <br />French,<br />Even Anglaise.<br /><br />At first, coy,<br />You held hands<br />With Neruda, <br />Until later, <br />No longer the<br />Sophisticated tease,<br />You gave yourself<br />To this man<br />Of simple ways,<br />Then both of you <br />Took off<br />Like swallows <br />On the breeze.<br /><br />You discovered<br />His thoughts, <br />His words, his love, <br />His passionate <br />Intensity<br />In a tiny book<br />That's now<br />Well-fingered.<br />Then you added your own<br />Unique voice, your arms<br />Your legs, your body <br />And your love,<br />So that in turn <br />You might be loved<br />And you were<br />And still, my heart, <br />Again, you are.<br /><br /><br /><br /><b>My Favourite Modern Lover<br />[Apologies to Jonathan Richman]</b><br /><br />Just a sketch, some paint, a glaze<br />Were all Picasso needed<br />To capture pretty women,<br />Some were even in the nude.<br />Although he was very short,<br />Girls could not resist his gaze.<br /><br />Then came Pablo Neruda.<br />He wrote lots of poetry.<br />He learned how to flirt with verse,<br />Now women who read it swoon.<br />Though he's my favoured Pablo,<br />I still can't tell who's ruder.<br /><br /><br /><b>Gratitude</b><br /><br />Thank you, Fate,<br />For guiding me <br />To my love.<br />The entanglement<br />Of limbs <br />And flesh<br />Is a pleasure<br />Great, but small<br />Compared with<br />When our<br />Hearts and minds<br />Enmesh.<br /><br /><br /><b>I'm in Love with a German Film Star<br />[I Crave Your Lips, Your Eyes, Your Avatar]</b><br /><br />Next time you're in bed<br />I'd like to hold you<br />In my loving arms,<br />Kiss you on the lips,<br />Run my hand over<br />Your curvaceous hips,<br />Gently part your legs,<br />Entreat your dew drops<br />And take a few sips,<br />Until you wanted <br />To be entered as<br />If we had been wed.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14886837209749464484" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_130848586" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/130848586" />
    <a title="Kitty" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4032367-kitty"><img alt="Kitty" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1419977972p2/4032367.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/130848586?book_show_action=true">Nov 15, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kitty" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kitty" href="/user/show/4032367-kitty">Kitty</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer130848586" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15411004435712761006">Neruda knew how to love a woman. There's such a sensuous, tactile quality to his poetry that makes you think he just might have been one hell of a lover. And mixed in with this earthy prose is an appreciation for the subtle, fleeting moments that last only in quick impressions and memories of wanting and desire. In one moment he tells us of the heavy weight and feel as he cups the rounded breasts of his mistress and the next he sighs his longing for the ability to devour the parts of her that li</span>
  <span id="freeText15411004435712761006" style="display:none">Neruda knew how to love a woman. There's such a sensuous, tactile quality to his poetry that makes you think he just might have been one hell of a lover. And mixed in with this earthy prose is an appreciation for the subtle, fleeting moments that last only in quick impressions and memories of wanting and desire. In one moment he tells us of the heavy weight and feel as he cups the rounded breasts of his mistress and the next he sighs his longing for the ability to devour the parts of her that linger in his memory whole. Truly the love poet for the modern age : <br /><br />I crave your mouth, your voice, your hair. <br />Silent, starving I prowl through the streets. <br />Bread does not nourish me, dawn disquiets me, <br />I search the liquid sound of your steps all day. <br /><br />I hunger for your sleek laugh, <br />For your hands the color of the wild grain, <br />I hunger for the pale stones of your fingernails, <br />I want to eat your skin like a whole almond. <br /><br />I want to eat the sunbeam flaring in your loveliness, <br />The nose, sovereign of your arrogant face, <br />I want to eat the fleeting shade of your lashes, <br /><br />And I walk hungry, smelling the twilight <br />Looking for you, for your hot heart, <br />Like a puma in the barren wilderness.<br /><br /><br />One thing I will say about this particular copy though is that while it is certainly the most comprehensive edition available to English speakers, it is not, perhaps, the best translated. Kudos to the editors for managing to put it all together though - and I do believe credit is due for not sticking to one translator for the whole thing. However when comparing certain works against others it becomes apparent that not all translators do their transcribing equally. Oh well, I suppose you have to go with what's available to you at the time. Obviously not all my favorites have done every single poem and I do appreciate the effort to use the superior translation when available. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15411004435712761006" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_139042961" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/139042961" />
    <a title="Jessaka" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4659464-jessaka"><img alt="Jessaka" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1485709659p2/4659464.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/139042961?book_show_action=true">Jan 07, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jessaka" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jessaka" href="/user/show/4659464-jessaka">Jessaka</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4659464-jessaka?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4659464-jessaka?shelf=nobel-prize-for-literature">nobel-prize-for-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer139042961" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2664915866078702326">Romance and War<br/><br/>I walked into Coalesce Bookstore in the quaint town of Morro Bay, CA and ended up in their poetry section. I saw Pablo Neruda’s name written on a book and pulled it off the shelf and began reading his poems on war, on politics. I put the book back on the shelf and began to walk out of the store. When I got to the door, I stopped, turned around and went back to read some more of his poems. I put it back on the shelf and began to walk away again. I stopped, grabbed the book and boug</span>
  <span id="freeText2664915866078702326" style="display:none">Romance and War<br /><br />I walked into Coalesce Bookstore in the quaint town of Morro Bay, CA and ended up in their poetry section. I saw Pablo Neruda’s name written on a book and pulled it off the shelf and began reading his poems on war, on politics. I put the book back on the shelf and began to walk out of the store. When I got to the door, I stopped, turned around and went back to read some more of his poems. I put it back on the shelf and began to walk away again. I stopped, grabbed the book and bought it. It was my introduction to Pablo Neruda, who won the Noble Prize for poetry. <br /><br />Over the years I have picked up this book to read a few poems, but I never really read them all. I did it now, but I am finding that they were more meaningful when I read only a few at a time.<br /><br />I began at the beginning of the book this time, with his love poems. It appeared to me that his love poems are as passionate as those of his on war and death. Either he is bursting with passionate love or in the deep despair of war. The love poems are filled with a touch of erotica. I felt that this man was more interested in making love to a woman than in knowing her soul. Perhaps, I should not judge, but at least I saw the beauty in each poem. I had never read them before, because I was only interested in his politics.<br /><br />As to his poems on war, they were depressing to me as I kept reading one after another. He must have written about every invasion in the in South America and in Spain. <br /><br />I wish to share one with you that contains both love and war:<br /><br />“…I have not left you when I go away.<br />Now I am going to tell you:<br />My land will be yours,<br />I am going to conquer it,<br />Not just to give it to you,<br />But for everyone,<br />for my people…<br />You will come with me to fight <br />face to face<br />because your kisses live<br />like red banners,<br />and if I fall, not only<br />Will earth cover me<br />but also this great love<br />that you brought me<br />and that lived circulating<br />in my blood.<br />You will come with me,<br />at that hour I wait for you,<br />at that hour and at every hour<br />I wait for you…<br />because I am a soldier…<br />My love, I wait for you in the<br />harshest desert<br />and next to the flowing<br />lemon tree,<br />in every place where<br />there is life, <br />where spring is being born…”</span>
  <a data-text-id="2664915866078702326" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_4005964" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/4005964" />
    <a title="Leila" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/249469-leila"><img alt="Leila" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1233734006p2/249469.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/4005964?book_show_action=true">Aug 02, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Leila" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Leila" href="/user/show/249469-leila">Leila</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">everyone</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/249469-leila?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer4005964" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1454178101864308062">breathtaking, heart wrenching, soul awakening -- Neruda is love ... <br/>"I do not love you as if you were salt-rose, or topaz,<br/>or the arrow of carnations the fire shoots off.<br/>I love you as certain dark things are to be loved,<br/>in secret, between the shadow and the soul.<br/><br/>I love you as the plant that never blooms<br/>but carries in itself the light of hidden flowers;<br/>thanks to your love a certain solid fragrance,<br/>risen from the earth, lives darkly in my body.<br/><br/>I love you without knowing how, or when, or from where.</span>
  <span id="freeText1454178101864308062" style="display:none">breathtaking, heart wrenching, soul awakening -- Neruda is love ... <br />"I do not love you as if you were salt-rose, or topaz,<br />or the arrow of carnations the fire shoots off.<br />I love you as certain dark things are to be loved,<br />in secret, between the shadow and the soul.<br /><br />I love you as the plant that never blooms<br />but carries in itself the light of hidden flowers;<br />thanks to your love a certain solid fragrance,<br />risen from the earth, lives darkly in my body.<br /><br />I love you without knowing how, or when, or from where.<br />I love you straightforwardly, without complexities or pride;<br />so I love you because I know no other way<br /><br />than this: where I does not exist, nor you,<br />so close that your hand on my chest is my hand,<br />so close that your eyes close as I fall asleep." <br /></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer16523739942523176011">I <b>Liked</b> Pablo Neruda's writing, I believe his raw passion speaks to all of us on a universal level. It's so human and bare, it is his monument left to us. This is an amazing collection which begins with his early work to his retrospective years, it shows you this amazing evolution of his writing and how powerful it becomes.   <span id="freeText16523739942523176011" style="display:none">I <b>Liked</b> Pablo Neruda's writing, I believe his raw passion speaks to all of us on a universal level. It's so human and bare, it is his monument left to us. This is an amazing collection which begins with his early work to his retrospective years, it shows you this amazing evolution of his writing and how powerful it becomes. Some poems really hit home where some of them really confused me. These poems really made this book a quite interesting read for me. <br /><br /><i>And I in these lines say:<br />Like this I want you, love,<br />love, Like this I love you,<br />as you dress<br />and how your hair lifts up<br />and how your mouth smiles,<br />light as the water<br />of the spring upon the pure stones,<br />Like this I love you, beloved..."<br />-Pablo Neruda</i><br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>I no longer love her, that's certain, but how I loved her.<br />My voice tried to find the wind to touch her hearing.<br /><br />Another's. She will be another's. Like my kisses before.<br />Her voice. Her bright body. Her inifinite eyes.<br /><br />I no longer love her, that's certain, but maybe I love her. <br />Love is so short, forgetting is so long.<br /><br />Because through nights like this one I held her in my arms<br />my soul is not satisfied that it has lost her.</i>
</blockquote><br /><br /><i>"I will bring you happy flowers from the mountains, bluebells, dark hazels, and rustic baskets of kisses. <br />I want to do with you what spring does with the cherry trees." <br /></i><br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>You discovered<br />His thoughts, <br />His words, his love, <br />His passionate <br />Intensity<br />In a tiny book<br />That's now<br />Well-fingered.<br />Then you added your own<br />Unique voice, your arms<br />Your legs, your body <br />And your love,<br />So that in turn <br />You might be loved<br />And you were<br />And still, my heart, <br />Again, you are.</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />This is definitely my first book of Pablo's but not the last. I am looking forward to his writing. <b>Highly Recommended to poetry &amp; Classic Lovers.</b> </span>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/11124253?book_show_action=true">Dec 27, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris M." class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris M." href="/user/show/436292-chris-m">Chris M.</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer11124253" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer756162420047206250">I'm not big on poetry. I've read the classics - Chaucer, Spenser, Shakespeare, Donne, etc. I've read the epic poems - Iliad, Odyssey, Gilgamesh. But modern era poetry usually leaves me cold--too much angst and unrequited love. However, I am always left floored by Neruda. Ode to Common Things got me into Neruda and remains one of my all time favorites. He is mostly famous for his love poems; and, while they are extraordinary, they are not IMHO his best. Neruda sees the epic and timeless connectio</span>
  <span id="freeText756162420047206250" style="display:none">I'm not big on poetry. I've read the classics - Chaucer, Spenser, Shakespeare, Donne, etc. I've read the epic poems - Iliad, Odyssey, Gilgamesh. But modern era poetry usually leaves me cold--too much angst and unrequited love. However, I am always left floored by Neruda. Ode to Common Things got me into Neruda and remains one of my all time favorites. He is mostly famous for his love poems; and, while they are extraordinary, they are not IMHO his best. Neruda sees the epic and timeless connections in...well...in everything. Spoons and salt shakers are the common man's connection with history and heaven. Love and life and death are found in unexpected places. I just received this volume of his poetry which covers most of his work, some of which I've read and some which I will certainly read over and over again. I wish I was fluent in Spanish so I could read this in the original as I have no doubt something is lost in the translation. Buty what is not lost is priceless.</span>
  <a data-text-id="756162420047206250" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer17818784217906659349">This book is the quintessential poetry book. Neruda is untouchable and this compilation is the best. If my house was burning and I could only run out with one book it would be a close call between this and Lorca's compilation. You could be stranded on a desert island with this book for the rest of your life and you would have a smile on your face. Y ahora, pido silencio.</span>

<div id="review_10900248" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/10900248" />
    <a title="Snehal Bhagat" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/362402-snehal-bhagat"><img alt="Snehal Bhagat" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1326107668p2/362402.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/10900248?book_show_action=true">Dec 22, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Snehal Bhagat" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Snehal Bhagat" href="/user/show/362402-snehal-bhagat">Snehal Bhagat</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer10900248" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17773867124942549346">An anthology of Pablo Neruda's poems translated into English. <br/><br/>With over 600 poems, this is a large and fairly representative collection covering sonnets, odes, cantos and free verse drawn from across the poet's entire career. I have been reading it on and off for a fairly long time but I still have not finished reading all of it, having skimmed through some parts and skipped others altogether primarily for two reasons: the difficulty, sometimes, in establishing the context, and the problems asso</span>
  <span id="freeText17773867124942549346" style="display:none">An anthology of Pablo Neruda's poems translated into English. <br /><br />With over 600 poems, this is a large and fairly representative collection covering sonnets, odes, cantos and free verse drawn from across the poet's entire career. I have been reading it on and off for a fairly long time but I still have not finished reading all of it, having skimmed through some parts and skipped others altogether primarily for two reasons: the difficulty, sometimes, in establishing the context, and the problems associated with the language barrier.<br /><br />There's always this thing about poetry and the milieu in which it is written- <a href="http://wonderingminstrels.blogspot.com/2003/02/visitor-les-murray.html" rel="nofollow noopener">some poems</a> draw only on shared human experience and so need little by way of a background- as with a great deal of love poetry, while <a href="http://wonderingminstrels.blogspot.com/2005/02/for-man-who-taught-tricks-to-owls-david.html" rel="nofollow noopener">for others</a>, the entire meaning can hinge on the reader's awareness or ignorance of its context; the former therefore tends to be more durable and has broader appeal. Neruda's love poetry is no exception - <a href="http://wonderingminstrels.blogspot.com/2000/05/sonnet-xvii-love-pablo-neruda.html" rel="nofollow noopener">it is exquisite</a> and extremely accessible; but I struggled with his epic poetry for I do not know enough of the context or the details of the provenance for quite a bit of it. <br /><br />The problem is persistent; even for a poem that purely describes nature, when we read '..and it is here that the world ends.' we can infer from the context that it is a place that is being spoken of and not a moment in time; but we do not know what place that is. Pedantic as it may seem, it matters whether the place the poet had in mind was off Cape Horn or the middle of the Atacama desert, especially because the verse is rooted so deeply in the land, its people and their traditions- often, both history and geography are intertwined to inform the narrative, so this isn't poetry that describes a continent as much as defines it: <br /><br /><i>The poisonous skin of the copper, the nitrate salt spread out <br />like a statue, crumbled and snowy: they're mine, but not <br />only them: also the vineyards, the cherries the spring rewards,<br /><br />they are mine too, and I belong to them, like a black atom<br />in the arid land, in the autumn light on the grapes,<br />in this metallic homeland lifted by towers of snow. </i><br /><br />Bards of course, are the bearers of literary ambrosia, but it is not merely when they concern themselves with the heroic canon that poets have the power to grant immortality; they can take everyday experiences and imbue them with deep significance, distill the wistful and extract the poignant, transform the merely special to the truly magical and in so doing make our ordinary lives a little less so.<br /><br />It is said of Neruda that he caresses words, and that there is great beauty in his use of the language; and doubtless this must be true, but though the original poem in Spanish often appears along with the English translation here, for those of us who do not understand both languages, there is no way to really appreciate it. And not knowing just how much the spirit of the original is captured in the translation can be frustrating too; but this is not to take away from the quality of the translations - even in the English versions there is no denying the moments when the breath comes unstitched and the heart misses a beat. <br /><br />The oeuvre of the poet who was the 'voice of the voiceless' is immensely prolific, yet incredibly versatile and so defies easy analysis. It goes through the entire spectrum- passionate, sad, tender, political, ornate, erotic, whimsical - witness the 'Ode to the Artichoke'- and at times dull and prosaic, but it is always original, and there are gems to be found even in his lesser known works. There is something charming about his politically motivated poetry too; quaint the anti-capitalistic tirades, Nixon-bashing and the Marxist espousal may seem now, but there is no mistaking the emotional intensity, the earnestness of feeling, the conviction of belief and the love of the utopian ideal that underlies it.<br /><br />This is a good book to pick up on a lazy afternoon and dip into at random, and I intend to revisit it again -and often, in the future; <i> yo volveré </i>- as the poet says.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17773867124942549346" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1052735156" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1052735156" />
    <a title="Dale Harcombe" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4800708-dale-harcombe"><img alt="Dale Harcombe" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1380932680p2/4800708.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1052735156?book_show_action=true">Sep 12, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dale Harcombe" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dale Harcombe" href="/user/show/4800708-dale-harcombe">Dale Harcombe</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1052735156" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11125545415713952507">Four and a half stars. I’m not sure how I managed to get through life to this point with only having read a couple of Pablo Neruda’s poems, so it was with great delight at a recent Lifeline book fair I picked up this single volume of his works, around 600 poems. Since I don’t read Spanish, I am reading them translated. But some also have the Spanish originals alongside them, which is interesting. <br/>As with any collection some poems appealed to me more than others. I’m not going to name them becaus</span>
  <span id="freeText11125545415713952507" style="display:none">Four and a half stars. I’m not sure how I managed to get through life to this point with only having read a couple of Pablo Neruda’s poems, so it was with great delight at a recent Lifeline book fair I picked up this single volume of his works, around 600 poems. Since I don’t read Spanish, I am reading them translated. But some also have the Spanish originals alongside them, which is interesting. <br />As with any collection some poems appealed to me more than others. I’m not going to name them because I suspect that next time I pick up this book and dip into the poems those favourites, like the tide, may have changed. And this is one book of poems that I have no doubt I will return to again and again.<br />Over all, I found the lucid writing, imagery and passion (and not just sexual passion) but passion for the land and for justice among other things drew me in. I would highly recommend this collection to anyone who does not read poetry normally as Neruda is largely an accessible poet with an eye for detail. Those who do read poetry will appreciate the skill involved in creating these poems. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11125545415713952507" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer2259803899" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16707645075062163474">a 1000 pager. Took me a year of it sitting bedside. </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2518226217" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2518226217" />
    <a title="Whiskey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/79191615-whiskey"><img alt="Whiskey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1521523346p2/79191615.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2518226217?book_show_action=true">Jan 21, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Whiskey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Whiskey" href="/user/show/79191615-whiskey">Whiskey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/79191615-whiskey?shelf=soul-mates">soul-mates</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/79191615-whiskey?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2518226217" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12128227511683009803"><i>“I love things with a wild passion, extravagantly.”</i> <br/>--Pablo Neruda<br/><br/><b>Pablo Neruda declared love with an inebriated tongue of flaming gold. The poet came from the people, and for them he sang. The poet molded ingots of song from mud, ash, and leaf-- into which he then sprinkled crushed emerald and lapis lazuli for ornament and for color. The mortar he mixed with sweat, blood, and uric acid. </b> <br/><br/>Pablo Neruda (1904-1973)-- the Chilean poet, exile, politician, and winner of the 1971 Nobel Prize-- <b>loved wi</b></span>
  <span id="freeText12128227511683009803" style="display:none"><i>“I love things with a wild passion, extravagantly.”</i> <br />--Pablo Neruda<br /><br /><b>Pablo Neruda declared love with an inebriated tongue of flaming gold. The poet came from the people, and for them he sang. The poet molded ingots of song from mud, ash, and leaf-- into which he then sprinkled crushed emerald and lapis lazuli for ornament and for color. The mortar he mixed with sweat, blood, and uric acid. </b> <br /><br />Pablo Neruda (1904-1973)-- the Chilean poet, exile, politician, and winner of the 1971 Nobel Prize-- <b>loved with a reckless abandon, and it spilled over into words—hundreds of thousands of glittering and loamy words. Four themes dominate his thousands of poems: love; nature; politics; and Neruda himself. But love is the leitmotif that links all themes as one. </b><br /><br /><b>1) Love</b><br />Most people will be attracted to Neruda’s love poems and songs of despair. Neruda was a lover of women, three of whom especially inflame his poetry. Much of the love poetry is tangled, moist, rich and earthy. This sonnet is one of my favorites: <blockquote> <i>I do not love you as if you were salt-rose, or topaz,<br />or the arrow of carnations the fire shoots off.<br />I love you as certain dark things are to be loved,<br />in secret, between the shadow and the soul.<br /><br />I love you as the plant that never blooms<br />but carries in itself the light of hidden flowers;<br />thanks to your love a certain solid fragrance,<br />risen from the earth, lives darkly in my body.<br /><br />I love you without knowing how, or when, or from where.<br />I love you straightforwardly, without complexities or pride;<br />so I love you because I know no other way<br /><br />than this: where I do not exist, nor you,<br />so close that your hand on my chest is my hand,<br />so close that your eyes close as I fall asleep. </i> </blockquote> Some of his love poems are explicitly sexual, and you will find many metaphors related to the secret organs of the earth: raucous roots piercing pungent soil; ships (vaginas), and swords, and sea foam. <blockquote>
  <i> Body of woman, white hills, white thighs,<br />You look like the world in your posture of surrender.<br />My savage peasant body digs through you<br />And makes the son leap from the depth of the earth.</i>
</blockquote> <b>But Neruda’s love poetry is tinged with sadness and despair: </b><blockquote><i>To feel you in my veins like God in the rivers and adore you in the sorrowful bones of dust and lime.<br /><br />Your breasts smell of honeysuckle while the sad wind goes slaughtering butterflies. </i> </blockquote> <b>2) Nature</b><br /><b>Let Neruda be your effusive tour guide into the heart of South American</b> mountains, pampas, jungles, and tundra. He wrote odes celebrating all of his continent's biodiversity and terrain and some were very long, Whitmanesque catalogs of flora and fauna. The biophilist in me responded to his many individual odes celebrating the earth and its land features and forms; its rocks and minerals; flora and fauna. Neruda transports you to the bird nests in the rainforest or along the Chilean coast. Listen to the shrieks of birds and dodge droppings as you read scores of odes devoted to different species: albatross; toucan; kestrel; seagull; cormorant; parakeet; gull; woodpecker; lapwing; mockingbird, and dodo bird. Likewise, Neruda loved rocks. He starts with an ode to the atom and proceeds to describe, in separate poems, the dozens of rocks and minerals unique to South America. <blockquote> <i><br />There is something dense, united, seated in the depths, <br />Repeating its number, its identical sign.<br />How clear it is that the stones have touched time,<br />In their fine substance there is a smell of age<br />And the water that the sea brings from salt and sleep.”</i>(32)</blockquote><br /><b>3) Politics</b><br />Neruda was a fearless political poet who endured years of exile because he would not shut up. Some readers expressly dislike his political poems, but he casts his lot with the oppressed and for him, silence was complicity with the oppressors. I encourage conservative readers to be patient and try to understand why Neruda was outraged at imperialism and colonialism-- at “the blood thirsty flies” who plundered Latin America's natural resources, exterminated indigenous culture, and supported kleptocratic capitalist dictators. <br /><br />Admittedly, I cringe when I see his handful of naive odes celebrating Stalin, Mao, and Castro—each of whom he later renounced when he learned of their butchery. <b> But on the whole, I think his political poems are another form of the love poetry-but, instead of erotic love for a woman, he proclaims love for the people and for social justice:</b> <blockquote> <i> Let it be known that nobody crossed my path without sharing my being.<br />I plunged up to the neck in adversities that were not mine,<br />Into all the sufferings of others. (680) <br /><br />Ah great love, small beloved!<br />I did not stop in the struggle.<br />I did not stop marching toward life,<br />Toward peace, toward bread for all.<br /><br />For my life, give me<br />All lives,<br />Give me all the sorrow<br />Of all the world<br />And I will transform it <br />Into hope. (364) </i> </blockquote><b> He was a prophets of justice like Neruda-- artists who venture outside self-absorption to take on the burdens of others and who risk disdain by proclaiming affiliations that will immediately alienate many readers. THey are political love poems to the people whom he saw as oppressed. </b><br /><br /><b>4) Personal</b><br />Neruda’s father was a provincial rail worker, and his mother died a few months after his birth. I see him as the Hispanic Walt Whitman. “I begin by evoking Whitman,” Neruda declares :<blockquote><i> Because I love my country<br /> I claim you, essential brother, <br />old Walt Whitman with your old gray hands. (825)<br /><br />I touched a hand that day,<br /> and it was Walt Whitman’s hand. <br />Throughout my entire childhood, <br />my companion was that hand with dew on it. <br />And Walt did not disdain <br />All the gifts of the earth <br />but raised my eyes <br />to the treasure we find <br />Inside a kernel of wheat.</i> (425) </blockquote> Neruda’s poems, like Whitman’s, are often catalogs of the various places he lived, the people he met, the flora and fauna that intrigued him, and the martyrs and poets who made him weep. <br /><br /><b>This Collection</b><br />Presented here are 600 poems, approximately a quarter of what Neruda wrote and which are ordered chronologically and organized by collection. The editor sprinkled in some untranslated Spanish poems; and occasionally gives alternate English translations of the same poem, which may surprise you at the tremendous difference a translator makes. Sometimes a poem that meant little to me in one translation leapt to life in another. <b> I can honestly say, I loved nearly every single poem in this collection. I highlighted something on almost every page, and I place this book on my altar bookcase, so that I may continually re-read Neruda with Whitman, Shakespeare and Montaigne for the rest of my life.</b> <br /><br /><b>My Conclusion</b><br /><b>This is my first review of poetry. A poet communicates experience in condensed and concentrated language. Neruda sings of multiplicity and of common things. In the words of the editor, Neruda “works from the bottom up.” <br /><br />I kneel with Neruda in the search for epiphany. I stand with him in rebellion against abstract, abstruse, evasive poetry. Irony and experimentalism have their place in poetry, but I prefer my poets-- passionate, simple, understandable, and honest. In Neruda’s heart there were furies and sorrows that he unleashes. He speaks for us that we might better speak for ourselves. </b></span>
  <a data-text-id="12128227511683009803" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_208449832" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/208449832" />
    <a title="Katie Joiner" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4325469-katie-joiner"><img alt="Katie Joiner" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1550583207p2/4325469.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/208449832?book_show_action=true">Sep 14, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Katie Joiner" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Katie Joiner" href="/user/show/4325469-katie-joiner">Katie Joiner</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer208449832" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3481702221447247165">Three words: Neruda. Is. Amazing. <br/>He is the best poet-or was, I guess-that this world has seen to date. He's become my new favorite! My family makes fun of me beacause I have something akin to a crush on him, but, hey, a girl can dream! <br/>His poetry is beautiful, captivating. I read a few every night before I go to bed, and it was perfect. I got sucked in. <br/>Okay, so you know how when you write something, or you read something, and there's this line that makes you go, "whoa" ? Usually it's the first</span>
  <span id="freeText3481702221447247165" style="display:none">Three words: Neruda. Is. Amazing. <br />He is the best poet-or was, I guess-that this world has seen to date. He's become my new favorite! My family makes fun of me beacause I have something akin to a crush on him, but, hey, a girl can dream! <br />His poetry is beautiful, captivating. I read a few every night before I go to bed, and it was perfect. I got sucked in. <br />Okay, so you know how when you write something, or you read something, and there's this line that makes you go, "whoa" ? Usually it's the first or last line. With Neruda, every other line is a holy-shiz-that's-the-perfect-way-to-say-that line. A why-didn't-i-think-of-putting-it-that-way stanza. A mesmerizing look inside the mind of a man who is a genius with words, but who is purely human with more insight than most upon the fact that he is human. <br />If you go through life without reading one of Neruda's "Odes to..." I feel sorry for you. Go. Offline. Read. Please. You won't regret it! </span>
  <a data-text-id="3481702221447247165" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2575626577" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2575626577" />
    <a title="Nuri" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/24205238-nuri"><img alt="Nuri" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1602277514p2/24205238.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2575626577?book_show_action=true">Nov 20, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nuri" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nuri" href="/user/show/24205238-nuri">Nuri</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24205238-nuri?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2575626577" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4235607008892054074">Neruda's poetry is absolutely <b>EXQUISITE.</b> As this was the first ever poetry book I picked up, I wasn't sure how to go through the comprehensive collection, so I hopscotched and finished Residence, Canto General and Isla Negra in the end as these looked more serious or long sections. (And my mind always like it easy and short).<br/>The following lines from "Ode to The Book I" strikes a chord in my heart and I believe Neruda lived what he wrote and he sure did love well. <br/><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>I’ve learned to take lif</i></b></blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText4235607008892054074" style="display:none">Neruda's poetry is absolutely <b>EXQUISITE.</b> As this was the first ever poetry book I picked up, I wasn't sure how to go through the comprehensive collection, so I hopscotched and finished Residence, Canto General and Isla Negra in the end as these looked more serious or long sections. (And my mind always like it easy and short).<br />The following lines from "Ode to The Book I" strikes a chord in my heart and I believe Neruda lived what he wrote and he sure did love well. <br /><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>I’ve learned to take life from life, <br />to love after a single kiss, <br />and I didn’t teach anything <br />to anyone except what I myself lived...</i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br /><u><b>One suggestion</b> to anyone, new to poetry like me, is to first read the notes glossed by Stavans about each book before you read the poems. I realized it later, but it is useful to know about the undercurrent of each book.</u> The editor has done a fantastic job and I would like to trust that nothing got lost in translation. Stavans has selected poems from <b>33 of Neruda's books</b> (listed below). The books include poems on the <i>common themes of love, sensuality, expressions on the art of loving and longing, despair, then, moves on to feelings of solitude and remoteness.</i> The collection then takes us through some compelling, <i>social and politically conscious poetry</i> - and <b>it's quite evident how Neruda was the poet of exile, the voice of displacement, the rebel poet.</b> The book has memoirs of sorrowful and painful events - <i>betrayal and bloodshed.</i> In various poems, Neruda recalls the children being bit, burnt, chained etc and there have been expressions to suggest that ultimately, brutality extricates the sufferers from their body. Truly gut wrenching! Neruda is obviously filled with sadness and nostalgia in these poems where he talks about the war, racial conflicts and it's aftermath. Nevertheless, he goes on to create a sense of justice and beauty. In the subsequent books, the reader sees a return or restoration to his former self, and the poetry becomes more and more personal on the themes of <i>lasting love, house, nature, birds, a healthy solitude, and looking at death with a kind of peace.</i> It is completely enthralling to read how Neruda has embraced in his poetry, the myriad of materials of this universe - sand, salts, stones, waves, wasps etc. <b>Love, beauty and empathy, however remains the leitmotifs of his poetry.</b> (He even wrote poems titled "To a Poor Dead Man," and 'The Dog Dies.' Deep and moving! In reading this book, one realizes they are not just reading poetry - but the poet's odyssey and of all those known to him- and each poem had a tale to tell which completely stirs you.<br /><br />Here's the list of books and some of my favorite poems from them- (I don't know how the reviews must be written, but a thing is the same kind of beautiful just once, so I thought it was imperative of me to put my impressions on this collection out there. In future, I will come back to it, with more layers to his poems unraveled)<br /><br />1. <b>Book of Twilight</b> - The starting poem itself blew me away, the lover is an adult and child all at once!<br />2. <b>Twenty Love Poems And A Song of Despair</b><br /><b>XX.</b><i>
  <blockquote>"I no longer love her, that's certain, but maybe I love her.<br />Love is so short, forgetting is so long."</blockquote>
</i><br />3. <b>Residence on Earth I AND II, Third Residence</b><br />This selection wasn't overall as per my taste but I did like it in parts. The poems are quite lyrical, written on the theme of solitude and remoteness.<br /><b>"Tyranny"</b> was a favorite from Residence I - <br /> <i>
  <blockquote>A time complete as an ocean, <br />a wound confused as a new being<br />encompass the stubborn root of my soul<br />biting the center of my security."</blockquote>
</i><br /><b>"Only Death"</b> from Residence II<br /><i>
  <blockquote>But death also goes through the world dressed as a broom,<br />she licks the ground looking for corpses,<br />death is in the broom,<br />it is death's tongue looking for dead bodies,<br />it is death's needle looking for thread.</blockquote>
</i><br />4. <b>Canto General</b><br />Ah! This collection was difficult to read, and more serious poetry, with some chilling account of suffering of children, chained and burned. This one line from <b>"The Liberators"</b> struck with me : <i>
  <b>"Your lips sealed by death are the purest entombed silence."</b>
</i><br />5. <b>The Captain's Verses</b><br />The most beautiful collection where he sees the earth in his lover, calls her a queen, sees her as clay for his potter hands. The poems are a dedication of his <i>blazing love</i> and passion for Matilde Urrutia. Neruda first published this collection in anonymity because he didn't want to hurt Urrutia's predecessor, Delia del Carril, who he admitted (in Memoirs) was his perfect mate for 18 years.<br />- <i>
  <b>"September 8"</b>
</i> is a favorite because it talks about "The Third Body/Thing" which poets like Donald Hall have talked as an 'essence' being shared between him and his wife, Jane Kenyon and, Robert Bly speaks of it in "A Man And A Woman Sit Near Each Other." <br />- <i>
  <b>"The Son"</b>
</i> is the most heart warming read on the birth of a son, but it goes beyond the phenomenon of birth itself. Go read!<br />- <i>
  <b>"Your Laughter"</b>
</i> is a poem loved by all. (...but do not take from me your laughter. Ah!)<br />6. <b>Elemental Odes</b><br />A stunning collection - one will be astonished at the <i>number of common things</i> Neruda was capable of writing odes to.<br />7. <b>Extravagaria</b><br />Favorites from this collection were <i>
  <b>"THE UNHAPPY WOMAN</b>
</i> (which really overwhelmed me) and <i>"Autumn Testament."</i><br />8. <b>Voyages and Homecomings.</b> <br />9. <b>One Hundred Love Sonnets</b> -My favorite was XC in Night.<br />10. <b>Song of Protest</b> - The only poem I grasped well was <i>"So Is My Life"</i> <br />11. <b>The Stones of Chile</b>-<br /><b>From</b> 'To the Traveler'<br /><i>
  <blockquote>"I know this at great cost, <br />that all life is not outward,<br />nor all death within"</blockquote>
</i><br />12. <b>Ceremonial Songs.</b> - The selection has poems on the theme of <i>torture, bloodshed and death.</i> <u>Women are the subject of those poems.</u><br />13. <b>Fully Empowered</b> - Most delightful read <i>
  <b>"To Wash A Child"</b>
</i><br />14. <b>Isla Negra</b> - About the many things that makes the Island beautiful, including his house. I loved Terusa I and II.<br />15. <b>Art of Birds</b> - (Mockingbirds, Albatross, Pelicans, Pigeons) - Neruda has learnt from all.<br />16. <b>A House in the Sand</b> - About his house on the Isla Negra, marked by his lover Urrutia's presence in the poem 'Love for this Book.'<br />17. <b>La Barcarola</b> - The Watersong Ends is a telling of his story to Matilde, about his travels through the jungles, in solitude, and awakening to a divinity and connectedness among all men. It is also reflective of a hope for utopianism.<br /><blockquote>
  <i>"we go on loving love <br />and in our blunt way <br />we bury the liars and live among the truth-tellers.</i>
</blockquote><br />18. <b>The Hands of Day</b> - <i>Guilty</i><br />19. <b>World's End</b> - <i>The Seeker</i><br />20. <b>Seaquake</b><br />21. <b>Still Another Day</b> - on the theme of solitude.<br />22. <b>The Flaming Sword</b> - XVIII 'Someone' about the first encounter of man and woman.<br /><blockquote>
  <i>He was so afraid, he found a woman. <br />She was like a hedgehog, <br />like a chestnut. <br />She was an edible being, <br />but man needed her. <br />The two were unique, <br />reborn from the earth <br />and fated for love or destruction.</i>
</blockquote><br />23. <b>Stones From The Sky</b> : Neruda writes about silence is intensified into a stone. He not only makes your thoughts revel about exquisite stones like the Ethiopian basalt, the emerald of Colombia or the quartz, but even the mundane dusty stones of the road. (Ah, I'm in awe of how he looked at the world, like nothing should be left out).<br />24. <b>Barren Terrain</b><br />25. <b>The Separate Rose</b><br />From Men II : <i>"We all arrive by different streets, <br /> by unequal languages, at Silence."</i><br />26. <b>A Call For The Destruction of Nixon and Praise for the Chilean Revolution</b> : The Judgment was a short yet a soul stirring poem.<br />27. <b>The Sea and the Bells</b><br />28. <b>2000</b> : Celebration of mankind's struggle to survive.<br />29. <b>Elegy</b><br />30. <b>The Yellow Heart</b><br />31. <b>Winter Garden</b> : <i>A Dog Has Died</i><br />32. <b>The Book of Questions</b><br />33. <b>Selected Failings</b> : <i> Sad Song To Bore Everyone </i><br /><br />The last section is called "Homage: Fourteen Other Ways of Looking at Pablo Neruda" where several translators have picked their favorite poems of Neruda. <br /><br />(25, 27, 29, 30, 31, 32, 33) were published posthumously.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4235607008892054074" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1516551194?book_show_action=true">Aug 17, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gloria Sun" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gloria Sun" href="/user/show/48125989-gloria-sun">Gloria Sun</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1516551194" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1186001161551475267">whew, i feel like i just finished an encyclopedia. this man is so human i think he feels more than i do, or at least he's better at putting it in words than i am, so that i become more sensitive to my condition. he's also incredibly versatile and talks about things from love to the history of the spanish conquest to nixon and watermelons. i also didn't know i had the ability to continuously read such a thick compilation of poetry - normally it's in smaller bursts, but now i have more confidence </span>
  <span id="freeText1186001161551475267" style="display:none">whew, i feel like i just finished an encyclopedia. this man is so human i think he feels more than i do, or at least he's better at putting it in words than i am, so that i become more sensitive to my condition. he's also incredibly versatile and talks about things from love to the history of the spanish conquest to nixon and watermelons. i also didn't know i had the ability to continuously read such a thick compilation of poetry - normally it's in smaller bursts, but now i have more confidence to do the same to other poets that i've had a taste of. wouldn't read again simply because there's no time, but it would be a treat to open up to a random page once in a while and take in what's there. i'm glad i own this.</span>

<div id="review_3534210857" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3534210857" />
    <a title="Joe Schmidt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/49697912-joe-schmidt"><img alt="Joe Schmidt" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3534210857?book_show_action=true">Sep 23, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joe Schmidt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joe Schmidt" href="/user/show/49697912-joe-schmidt">Joe Schmidt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780374299958" href="/book/show/1317399.The_Poetry_of_Pablo_Neruda">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3534210857" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6864005805909145015">This was a lot for me. Though I enjoy the occasional poem (especially as a quote at the start of a story or chapter), 1000 pages was just too much for a single volume for me. I know that I got impatient because I love the continuity of a narrative, but his poetry is so stirring and picturesque I can forgive Neruda. Smaller poetry doses moving forward.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_775256747" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/775256747" />
    <a title="Michelle Marcano" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18743150-michelle-marcano"><img alt="Michelle Marcano" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1364308683p2/18743150.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/775256747?book_show_action=true">Nov 27, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michelle Marcano" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michelle Marcano" href="/user/show/18743150-michelle-marcano">Michelle Marcano</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18743150-michelle-marcano?shelf=reading-like-a-writter">reading-like-a-writter</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer775256747" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17321424108954899890">Pablo Neruda is a Spanish poet. I came across one of his poems on pintrest and since then have been absolutely obsessed with it. I have read it in Spanish and in English and it translate beautifully in Spanish. I have attached the poem below. The use of repetition in this poem really adds to the emotions behind it. It has such great details that describ the main idea of the poem. I really think that this poem was written to describe that the best kind of love is that love that one can't explain </span>
  <span id="freeText17321424108954899890" style="display:none">Pablo Neruda is a Spanish poet. I came across one of his poems on pintrest and since then have been absolutely obsessed with it. I have read it in Spanish and in English and it translate beautifully in Spanish. I have attached the poem below. The use of repetition in this poem really adds to the emotions behind it. It has such great details that describ the main idea of the poem. I really think that this poem was written to describe that the best kind of love is that love that one can't explain or describe where it comes from. The use of repetition on the poem really help the readers understand the poem. I think that very often poems are so complex that it is sometimes hard to understand, but Neruda's poem uses so much repetition that it is easy to understand exactly what the poem is about. I also really love how he starts his poem by using the words "I do not love you" I think that this part sets the flow of the poem because by reading those words we think the poem will be about something completely different than what it was. In every stanza of the poem Neruda uses repetition to emphasize on how much he loves this person. I also really enjoyed the similes and metaphors he uses in order to make the poem interesting. I would love to use poetry with repetition and metaphors in my classroom. I think that poetry is hard for children to understand and therefore is something that they don't like to do. I think that by showing children that poetry is simply about writing what they truly feel using great details I will be able to have a classroom full of poets. <br /><br />Sonnet XVII<br /><br />I do not love you as if you were salt-rose, or topaz,<br />or the arrow of carnations the fire shoots off.<br />I love you as certain dark things are to be loved,<br />in secret, between the shadow and the soul.<br /><br />I love you as the plant that never blooms<br />but carries in itself the light of hidden flowers;<br />thanks to your love a certain solid fragrance,<br />risen from the earth, lives darkly in my body.<br /><br />I love you without knowing how, or when, or from where.<br />I love you straightforwardly, without complexities or pride;<br />so I love you because I know no other way<br /><br />than this: where I does not exist, nor you,<br />so close that your hand on my chest is my hand,<br />so close that your eyes close as I fall asleep. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17321424108954899890" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('zWJcFPGCDQZfrT4mHGj1SPZVDU9b8f9Xa1p+aFDQ5R73RDXGStzEbviGikfrnf+XIArqh5V8cTAwJDjZnZMXhA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('8P7SyUuiqS7wif/wQv2LqEGOfm9l+N8Ju1NMHcuOpcPK2Lsb8PxgRleiS5G1CIF3l9GZp6t1UW7gLQqsBs1XWQ==')})
  }

Lorca (1898-1937) is admired all over the world for the lyricism, immediacy and clarity of his poetry, as well as for his ability to e<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText12029263183344074081\" style=\"display:none\">The Selected Poems of Federico García Lorca has introduced generations of readers to mesmerizing poetry since 1955. Lorca (1898-1937) is admired all over the world for the lyricism, immediacy and clarity of his poetry, as well as for his ability to encompass techniques of the symbolist movement with deeper psychological shadings. But Lorca\'s poems are, most of all, admired for their beauty. Undercurrents of his major influences--Spanish folk traditions from his native Andalusia and Granada, gypsy ballads, and his friends the surrealists Salvador Dali and Luis Bunuel--stream throughout Lorca\'s work.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/4026.Pablo_Neruda">About Pablo Neruda</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/4026.Pablo_Neruda"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1499982432p3/4026.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/4026.Pablo_Neruda">Pablo Neruda
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
7,730
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer16131855968309960474">Pablo Neruda was the pen name and, later, legal name of the Chilean writer and politician Neftalí Ricardo Reyes Basoalto. Neruda assumed his pen name as a teenager, partly because it was in vogue, partly to hide his poetry from his father, a rigid man who wanted his son to have a "practical" occupation. Neruda's pen name was derived from Czech writer and poet Jan Neruda; Pablo is thought to be fro</span>
  <span id="freeText16131855968309960474" style="display:none">Pablo Neruda was the pen name and, later, legal name of the Chilean writer and politician Neftalí Ricardo Reyes Basoalto. Neruda assumed his pen name as a teenager, partly because it was in vogue, partly to hide his poetry from his father, a rigid man who wanted his son to have a "practical" occupation. Neruda's pen name was derived from Czech writer and poet Jan Neruda; Pablo is thought to be from Paul Verlaine. With his works translated into many languages, Pablo Neruda is considered one of the greatest and most influential poets of the 20th century.<br /><br />Neruda was accomplished in a variety of styles, ranging from erotically charged love poems like his collection Twenty Poems of Love and a Song of Despair, surrealist poems, historical epics, and overtly political manifestos. In 1971 Neruda won the Nobel Prize for Literature, a controversial award because of his political activism. Colombian novelist Gabriel García Márquez once called him "the greatest poet of the 20th century in any language."<br /><br />On July 15, 1945, at Pacaembu Stadium in São Paulo, Brazil, he read to 100,000 people in honor of Communist revolutionary leader Luís Carlos Prestes. When Neruda returned to Chile after his Nobel Prize acceptance speech, Salvador Allende invited him to read at the Estadio Nacional before 70,000 people.<br /><br />During his lifetime, Neruda occupied many diplomatic posts and served a stint as a senator for the Chilean Communist Party. When Conservative Chilean President González Videla outlawed communism in Chile, a warrant was issued for Neruda's arrest. Friends hid him for months in a house basement in the Chilean port of Valparaíso. Later, Neruda escaped into exile through a mountain pass near Maihue Lake into Argentina. Years later, Neruda was a close collaborator to socialist President Salvador Allende.<br /><br />Neruda was hospitalized with cancer at the time of the Chilean coup d'état led by Augusto Pinochet. Three days after being hospitalized, Neruda died of heart failure. Already a legend in life, Neruda's death reverberated around the world. Pinochet had denied permission to transform Neruda's funeral into a public event. However, thousands of grieving Chileans disobeyed the curfew and crowded the streets to pay their respects. Neruda's funeral became the first public protest against the Chilean military dictatorship.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16131855968309960474" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

<div class="clear"></div></div></div><div class="bigBoxBottom"></div></div>
